B0072IVGRY|1.0|This is not a review of the music itself, but rather the sound quality.<br /><br />I was really looking forward to this LP as well as the new Van Halen LP, so I pre-ordered the LPs as soon as they were available.  I should have waited.  I would have then found out the mastering of both albums removed essentially all dynamic range, and they used that same master to press on vinyl. For instance, listen to this album on a decent sound system, and then listen to Electric right after...night and day.  Choice of Weapon is muddled and compressed with no separation of the instruments and is very ear fatiguing.  The human ear gets fatigued when it hears the same sound at the same level for a period of time.  Electric on the other hand is engaging and tasty due to the peaks and valleys of the sound.  The quiet passages in the music are quiet, and the loud passages of the music are loud.  You can clearly hear and feel the drums, because it was mastered with the dynamic range needed to allow the listener to hear proper separation and full range of the instruments. This is the main reason the human ear finds music pleasing.  Sounds made at the same sound level over periods of time displease the human ear. Anyone remember a teacher who spoke with no inflection in their voice?  The same monotone level?  Not a very interesting class I bet, regardless of the quality of content.<br /><br />It is obvious that overall the market does not demand sound quality with full dynamic range. I am not suggesting this should change or will change. Technology, cost, and convenience will often trump the time and costs required to obtain quality. I am similar in almost all ways except for how I prefer to listen to music. If someone can hold thousands of free songs in a cool gadget in the palm of their hand, it is certainly going to change the way people listen to their music. Instead of spending $15 on an album and listening to the music in a room with their friends on a high quality system, they are going to pop in their earbuds or turn up their 2.1 computer speakers and listen to a song for free on Youtube.  As a result, the sound attributes of the music should then match the primary method of which it is listened. So it is understandable that today's music is mastered accordingly, resulting in very compressed sounding music which is arguably more appropriate for low fidelity playback, and how people now generally listen to music.<br /><br />But then why vinyl?<br /><br />Granted, the vinyl \\"resurgence\\" is not going to make record companies millions, nor is vinyl ever going to become the main playback medium again, and nor should it. However it is a medium that has a dedicated niche following (3+ million new LPs sold in 2011) who appreciate sound quality.  As a result, these folks are more than willing to pay $20-$30 for a vinyl LP instead of downloading the album for free. It would be great if record companies and artist would think more long term on the vinyl mini-resurgence.  They could promote people's willingness to actually pay for music, by creating a separate master with full dynamic range for the vinyl pressing and market it accordingly as the medium made for sound quality, justifying the higher price tag (examples:  White Stripes, Black Keys). Instead of taking the same no dynamic range master created for ear buds plugged into an I-Phone and slapping it on vinyl for $15-$20, charge $20-$30 for a vinyl pressing with a separate master made with full dynamic range.  Folks already into vinyl would gladly pay this price, and it would also lead to more people in the future discovering vinyl and sound quality, allowing for continued vinyl LP sales success and growth.<br /><br />For the purpose of context, I am not some disgruntled 50-60 year old who simply refuses to let go of his vinyl collection in favor an IPOD. I am from the CD and MP3 generation, and for about 10 years I was not all that into music. I then decided to see what the vinyl fuss was all about four years ago.  Because of vinyl, I discovered that my lack of interest in music was due to the fact that MP3s and compressed audio made the music less tangible and less engaging.  It could no longer keep my interest. So if the practice of putting lipstick on pig by taking masters made for low fidelity listening and pressing them on vinyl continues, the vinyl resurgence will surely fade. People who are into vinyl like myself will not buy any new music on vinyl, and the new listeners will never have a reason to pay for music, since it will still sound like garbage on vinyl and you can't play an LP on a Facebook. Then alas, the record companies will have destroyed a sure fire way of getting at least some consumers to actually purchase music.  Sounds like history repeating itself.bout four years ago. 0307719219|5.0|When it comes to explaining why some nations are rich and others poor, you can hear a lot of theories, some possible, others crackpot.  People love to point their fingers at geography or climate, at culture or religion (especially if it's not THEIR religion), or at pure ignorance.  Other, more thoughtful theorists have developed extensive arguments for `modernization' or at least the need to tinker with existing institutions or to tolerate dictatorships which may lead to eventual reforms.  The USA and other developed nations have spent untold billions since 1945 in trying to lift the nations of the so-called Third World out of poverty. Aid to countries where corrupt elites siphon it off or where organization is weak is mostly, if not completely, lost.<br /><br />If you undertake to read WHY NATIONS FAIL you will get a very coherent argument as to why aid is often useless and why some nations fail while others get rich.  Why did the Industrial Revolution take off in England first ?  Why did Russia stagnate after 1970 ?  What was the economic result of desegregation in the US South ?  Why has Somalia fallen apart ?  To follow the arguments you will have to remember a few (very few) terms that are used by the authors to put across their ideas.  Two are \\"inclusive economic and political institutions\\" and \\"extractive economic and political institutions\\".  That is, some political institutions include a large number of groups and a broad spectrum of people and some economic institutions work to raise the welfare of large numbers of people in a particular country.  Others are run by a small, elite group in their own interests and only a small number (the same people) benefit.  \\"Creative destruction\\" means that while technological progress may put some people out of business (think slide rule or carbon paper manufacturers), the new technology benefits an even wider section of the population, thus being `creative'.  \\"Critical juncture\\" is their most jargonistic term, meaning a moment in history when things change and could go in several, different ways.  Armed with these terms, you'll be able to read this most ably-written and interesting book.  What I liked most about the book is its incredibly wide number of historical portraits drawn from all parts of the world, from the USA, Mexico, and Guatemala to Russia, China, Ethiopia, Somalia, Botswana and Sierra Leone, not forgetting Australia.  This is only a partial listing of examples.  The maps are also very useful.  What I liked least about the book is that the authors hammer you with the same set of ideas over and over.  I felt like shouting, \\"OK, OK, I get it !\\"  The length could have been cut with that in mind.  Whether or not Acemoglu and Robinson are totally correct, I can't say, not being so widely-versed in such theories.  In some ways it feels a bit reductive.  However, I guarantee that if you finish this book, you'll never look at foreign aid in the same way.  You may wind up with a number of questions about US foreign policy too.  And that's not bad.a moment in history when things change and could go in several, different ways.  Armed with these terms, you'll be able to read this most ably-written and interesting book. I was somewhat skeptical to try out the Savarez Alliance set, as carbon tends to produce a brighter treble and the measured tension is higher than the LaBellas.. I was wrong to assume that a lower tension (normal tension for both sets) would also imply that the strings would be easier to fret as well.. The Alliance AR500 strings seem to have more flexibility and brightness.. The next test is to determine their longevity.. These strings sound wonderfully balanced on a cedar top, are a bit brighter in the bass, but very well balanced.. try them out.. you won't be disappointed. It's also noteworthy to mention that the smaller diameter E and B trebles improve upon the string distance between each string.. this benefit provides a little more room to play in-between the spacings with regard to the right hand techniques.. I also do not find that the smaller diameters feel strange.

0385341008|3.0|The Geurnsey Literary and Potato Peel Pie Society is a pleasant, enjoyable read. I don't think it's great literature, but it's not trash, either.  It may even be mostly fluff, but that's not necessarily a bad thing.<br /><br />Set shortly after the close of hostilities in WWII, the book is an epistolary novel, i.e., written as a series of letters. The bulk of the letters are between Juliet Ashton, author, and assorted residents of Guernsey, though other writers and recipients appear. Likewise, some exchanges are brief notes and even telegrams to and from other people in Juliet's life.<br /><br />The overall tone of the book is light, leading me to believe that a line that struck me was no accident.  John Booker writes, in his first letter to Juliet, that he has been reading the letters of Seneca, \\"a Roman philosopher who wrote letters to imaginary friends telling the how to behave for the rest of their lives.  Maybe that sounds dull, but the letters aren't--they're witty.  I think you learn more if you're laughing at the same time.\\"<br /><br />That struck me, maybe because that's why I like to--no, it's not that I like to be funny, but that I can't  help seeing the humor in just about everything (including times and events when it's not convenient).  But I had a strong feeling that Ms. Schaffer agrees with Booker, that whatever you want to say, you'll be heard more surely if you say it with laughter. or at least lightness. I don't think that the book has a  \\"message,\\" but it does seem to be saying that whatever you face, better to face it with whatever humor you can muster. And with friends and books, not necessarily in that order.<br /><br />The underlying story about how the Guernsey islanders endured the Nazi Occupation--and how at least some discovered that, whatever evil Nazism held, individual Germans were very apt to still be humans--is strong and appealing.  Juliet's discovery of the Island and Islanders and her growing affection is also appealing, if not 100% real.  For me, the love story doesn't measure up.  It felt predictable and unconvincing. The \\"negative love story,\\" Juliet's interactions with the man she is clearly not destined to marry, is pleasant and humorous, if not wholly believable.  It's part of the humor of the story and as such is worthwhile.<br /><br />The story of True Love is charming and I rooted for it, but I didn't really believe in it.  I could see it coming a mile away, and never felt either doubt that it would  happen or any real belief that it made sense.  It didn't make me hate the book, but it did knock some points off.<br /><br />Overall, The Guernsey Literary and Potato Peel Pie Society is a grand read, taught me a bit I never knew about WWII, and feels like a book I might return to, as I do to many easy reads, when chocolate isn't enough. When other people might turn on the TV for reruns of The Wonder Years, I re-read books that are like mental comfort food. Ms. Staffer's book might well qualify for that list, and maybe that's high enough praise.  3 1/2 stars.0% real.  For me, the love story doesn't measure up.  It felt predictable and unconvincing. 0307730697|4.0|I was fortunate enough to read the book Fearless by Eric Blehm, a story about Adam Brown, officially known as a Chief Special Warfare Operator (SEAL), one of the most respected Special Ops warriors in the US Navy. He worked for the Naval Special Warfare Development Group (NSWDG), aka DEVGRU. The story begins with anecdotes about Adam's life, his struggle with drug addiction and his life prior to joining the armed forces, all of which contributed to the larger picture of his life story. Blehm does an excellent job of conveying Adam's devotion to family, friends, and his faith. Toward the end of the story the reader understands how Adam's drive, love of country, and desire to serve others eclipsed his physical ailments, emotional struggles, and brought him to serve and die for his country. This story is inspiring, emotional, and a wonderful testament of God's providence. I was privileged to read this book and I will certainly be passing it on to others.

B000FC0SIM|5.0|On Writing is not just for the aspiring writer--but for anyone who loves books and reading. This book will help the aspiring writer spot key issues in his/her work and how to address them. For the reader, it will make you appreciate the craft and the work (good) writers go through from beginning to end. Not only that, but this book will also help you spot those stories that aren't quite up-to-par (lazy writing, repetitiveness, lack of character development, useless words, etc...). This is not a step-by-step guide for aspiring writers (King makes this clear), but what works for Stephen King himself.<br /><br />What I really liked about this book is that King not only discusses writing, but also key points of his life--which I found very interesting. He discusses his childhood and the beginning points of his writing, meeting his wife, the struggles of being an aspiring writer, selling his first novel (Carrie), drug abuse, and being hit by a van in 1999.<br /><br />I found myself not wanting to put this book down until I was done (although I eventually had to due to lack of sleep). I practically hung on every word. I can pretty much guarantee I will never be able to attend a King seminar, or be able to interview him in person, so this book was the next best thing. It's written in a way that makes you feel as if you are listening to him speak right in front of you. On Writing is amazing stuff!

John Carter is based on the first two Barsoom books by Edgar Rice Burroughs, a wildly popular novelist of the late nineteenth and early twentieth century who is best known for the Tarzan novels. His sci-fi fantasy/adventure series was less well known in its time, but like the Tarzan novels is still read.<br /><br />John Carter follows the plot and character development of A Princess of Mars and Gods of Mars far more faithfully than I expected. A key element of this heaviness is the tonal palette Raskulinecz and the band chose to apply during engineering and mixing.  Another is Alex's sound--some of the fullest, gutsiest guitar presence I think I've ever heard from Rush.<br /><br />Apparently some object to the album's sound, calling it \\"cloudy,\\" \\"muddy,\\" etc.  I think I know why. B007I2BZIE|5.0|It's safe to say that no matter how good a band's later-era work is, it will never quite be as fresh and urgent as their \\"classic releases\\" earlier in their career.  There will always be that missing element of youthful self-discovery, or rapid evolution.  That being said, Rush's \\"Clockwork Angels\\" still ranks as a true return to form.  Having discovered Rush as a college freshman entering his second semester in early 1991, I missed out on hearing the band's \\"prime sequence\\" releases as they came out (with the possible exception of having seen the band's video for \\"Distant Early Warning\\" back in my youth).  In that sense, though, I was lucky to be able to judge the band's work to date all at the same time, and I was quite excited to discover this band that seemed to take all kinds of mainstream rock influences, and mix them all into a great, career-spanning sound that never felt contrived.  Sadly, it seems that none of the \\"new Rush album debuts\\" that I'd bear witness to as a fan could live up to my initial excitement in early 1991.  The nadir would have to be the midnight release of \\"Test For Echo\\", in the mid-1990s, where it seemed not one of the songs made any kind of impression on me in the slightest (although in retrospect, some of the songs did manage to stand the test of time).<br /><br />\\"Clockwork Angels\\", thankfully, brings with it at least a fair measure of that intangible \\"excitement\\" I was craving.  It's not just the hunger for Rush to return to prog-rock conceptualism, a la \\"2112\\" or the \\"Cygnus X-1\\" songs, that produces that sensation; it's that for the first time in a long time, Rush seems to not be holding back, and is really conveying a sense of their own passion through the structure and performance of these songs.  While prior studio release \\"Snakes and Arrows\\" had some pretty good cuts (such as \\"Far Cry\\", \\"Armor and Sword\\", and \\"The Main Monkey Business\\"), there was still some of that leftover \\"polite restraint\\" that was far too dominant in \\"Test For Echo\\", albeit with a decent measure of energy starting to kick back in.  Long story short, the album succeeds in large part because the band is utilizing one of their key strengths: writing really catchy songs that are complex, but still pay some respect to traditional song structures, and performing those songs like they mean every second of it, and then some.<br /><br />Don't approach this album expecting songs that are fully in the vein of their more complex work, even though there are still more shades of the kind of music on \\"A Farewell To Kings\\" or \\"Permanent Waves\\" than there have been in over 2 decades.  The title track exudes the sort of shimmery, ethereal spaciness of \\"Xanadu\\", but in a more straightforward manner, while Alex Lifeson's soloing in \\"Caravan\\" will remind the listener of his off-kilter breakdown in \\"La Villa Strangiato\\".  Yes, Alex's guitar solos are definitely more frequent here than they've been in quite a while, but just as important is a departure from the \\"sonic dogma\\" approach that would typify certain albums.  Instead of insisting on focusing on keyboards, leaving out keyboards entirely, having an ultra-light sound, or going for a dirty, gritty sound, the album is well-layered, with the different instruments and the vocals all contributing to a bigger picture.  And while the band can kick it out bigtime with the expected syncopated power-riffing in \\"Headlong Flight\\", the aforementioned \\"Caravan\\", or \\"BU2B\\", it's the surprisingly high quality of hidden gems such as the poorly-named, but awesome \\"Carnies\\" (which packs a lot of shifting dynamics into its run time) and the incredibly dark yet catchy and lush \\"The Wreckers\\" (which channels a bit of the spirit of \\"Afterimage\\") that seals the deal.<br /><br />While I'm still debating whether this is their best album since \\"Counterparts\\", since \\"Presto\\" or since \\"A Show of Hands\\" (my first CD!), I should note that the album isn't perfect.  While not a bad song, \\"Seven Cities of Gold\\" rides the same riff far too often for an over 6 minute song (do love the cowbell though).  Similarly, \\"Wish Them Well\\" isn't poorly written or performed, but seems to be satisfied just being a vehicle for the lyrical content, as opposed to really grabbing the listener.  That being said, the fact that these are the weakest songs on \\"Clockwork Angels\\" speaks volumes of the overall quality of the experience.  I should also note that the lyrics deserve to be credited with managing to both succeed at providing exposition for the main character's story arc, and at succeeding in artfully exploring themes that are relevant to all of us: individuality, bitterness, earned pessimism, forgiveness, mortality, and much more.  It's to the band's credit that they've managed a difficult feat: creating a concept album with a defined story that manages to avoid many of the pitfalls of that type of work.  Then again, I'd expect nothing less from Rush....and long may they continue to meet those standards!

There is absolutely no question when you are watching \\"Tree of Life\\" you are watching a Malick film.<br /><br />But I must also confess that his films are demanding. They're like reading Melville or Joyce. Or listening to Mahler. You have to experience them more than once to appreciate all the subtlety and apparent meaning. And you have to be paying attention. \\"Tree of Life\\" is no exception. For instance, it is not clear, until the end, which son is the narrator, making the movie a mystery in the tradition of [[ASIN:B005MMY5RK Citizen Kane (70th Anniversary)]]or [[ASIN:B001H1SVO8 Fight Club]]. The narrative is non-linear, and the editing feels, at first, random and jarring, until you get into the unique time-space that marks a Malick universe. And the relationship of Brad Pitt and Jessica Chastain, as husband and wife, is also ambiguous and at times frighteningly tense, as a child's perception of his own parents' marriage often is. All of this requires getting used to. And more than one viewing.<br /><br />For any man with a father, \\"Tree of Life\\" is a strongly affecting movie. I found it hard to watch without choking up. While it was a very different style of movie, the theme of [[ASIN:B0001GOH6Q Big Fish]], Tim Burton's own monument to the complex relationship of father and son, was on my mind as I watched Tree of Life. They were, in one sense, the same film. At least they leave you with the same questions about yourself and your father.

B006E2VTG2|1.0|This film is unconvincing and unbelievable. If you like to watch a pretty girl, then go right ahead. Your heroine is presented as invincible, right out of the gate. There is no learning curve, no set of challenges for her to overcome...and thus no suspense whatsoever. Mostly she just wriggles around, getting in and out of places where she's not supposed to be. Action fans, take note: there isn't a real fight scene until the final 15 minutes, and it's nothing worth waiting around for. But Zoe always manages to look good, and that's the whole point of a vanity picture.<br /><br />Then there's the abysmal plot. Zoe plays a young woman who lost her family, when she was a girl. All grown up now - with some training from a family relation - she wants revenge. But while she's told to be a smart assassin, she \\"signs\\" every victim, which should make her easy to find. Could bad things happen to good people around her, because of this? Sure. Thus, through her own stupidity, Zoe's character creates another reason to exact revenge. But since every other character in the film is clueless, too, this takes some time - 20 bodies - yet it's as inevitable as every other predictable detail here. What's more, there was apparently no money left for casting. So we get Michael Vartan, completely miscast and unlikely as the artist/boyfriend. There's zero chemistry there. Perhaps the whole thing should be viewed as an absurdist take on the revenge picture. But don't take my word for it: check out the rottentomatoes website, and you'll see that only 27% of the pro reviewers liked it, and only 53% of audiences. The 4 and 5 star reviews I see on Amazon just don't line up with reality. There are some great female revenge flicks out there -- Hanna and Haywire are two. And don't forget Death Proof. Of course, serious action and fighting girl fans will go back to La Femme Nikita. Check all these great films out, and leave this unconvincing nonsense behind.<br /><br />Incidentally, there are other reviewers here who seem to love this film. Their main protest against more critical commentary is \\"This is just a movie! It's not a documentary.\\" This is flawed reasoning. I would challenge these reviewers to start thinking about what they're watching. Directors have roughly two hours to present a coherent, original piece of moviemaking - a clear time, place, perspective and cast which all function cohesively. Their success can be judged by how satisfied we are with the final product. Colombiana is neither original nor satisfying, on several levels. I think you will agree.

B00007GZR5|4.0|Whenever Robin Williams stars in a film that is out of the ordinary of his comedic roles, he shows what acting is about.  \\"What Dreams May Come\\" is one of the examples of Williams's diverse filmography that surpasses the humor he is so well know for.  The film focuses on how one handles grief and loss, but what is unique is how it is approached -- much like an artist's blank canvas.  As personal expressions are interpreted within the canvas in the form of colorful and emotional images, one realizes that the memories and the images can forever be embedded within the frame of a canvas.  For Williams's character Chris, that appears to be the case but not quite coincidental because his wife Annie (Annabella Sciorra) was an artist.  Chris and Annie's subject on the canvas is coming to terms with their children's death.<br /><br />Through present events and flashbacks before the loss, Director Vincent Ward shows that Chris and Annie lived an average life.  Their life changes after their children's accident and the quick turn of events that have Chris, especially Annie struggling to overcome their grief.  Certain parts of the film may pay reminders to other films that depict characters encountering or about to encounter life changing moments, \\"It's A Wonderful Life,\\" \\"City of Angels,\\" \\"Heaven Can Wait,\\" and \\"Meet Joe Black.\\"  Indeed, all of these films possess a metaphysical quality, but \\"What Dreams May Come\\" may stand out from all of the above.  Chris appears to be in limbo between heaven and hell, and it is where he first ends up that looks like paradise - his wife's paintings, beautiful and celestial.  However, he is not alone in his journey and is accompanied by the first doctor that he had interned with, Albert (Cuba Gooding, Jr.).  And one wonders of the significance of each and every character or events that Chris experiences, which may make the viewer think beyond appearances.  Ward is very suggestive of the storyline that delves much deeper into the examination of natural order and choice and how it relates to how individuals deal with loss.<br /><br />\\"What Dreams May Come\\" is thought provoking and questions may arise throughout the film and after.  So many words can describe the story being told within the film, but one phrase comes to mind, life is like a painting.f natural order and choice and how it relates to how individuals deal with loss.<br /><br />\\"What Dreams May Come\\" is thought provoking and questions may arise throughout the film and after. 0393088774|5.0|End This Depression Now by Paul Krugman Nobel Laureate in Economics<br /><br /> Paul Krugman presents evidence that to end this fiscal recession--which he calls depression because of lack of jobs and that is going on on four years and an increase in income inequality--we need new fiscal policies.  A fiscal stimulus that helps the economy to add jobs--contrary to what some people will say, the first stimulus did not work because it was not strong enough--and that to reduce the deficit lowers growth, especially in the near term.<br /><br /> The Book specifically discusses two different and opposing points of view: the first, preached by John Maynard Keynes' 1936 work, General Theory of Employment, Interest and Money<br /> \\"The outstanding faults of economic society in which we live are its failure to provide for full employment and its arbitrary an inequitable distribution of wealth and incomes.\\" John Maynard Keynes'  General Theory of Employment, Interest and Money<br /><br />Although income inequality is hard to solve, unemployment is not. Krugman cites the example of the US entry into WWII where a burst of federal spending cause a 7% rise in the total number of jobs in America.  The equivalent of adding more than 10 million jobs today.  Krugman supports his beliefs in this policy by the Paul Samuelson textbook, Economics first published in 1948 which brought Keynesian economics to American colleges.<br /><br /> The second opposing theory is championed by Milton Friedman.  Friedman's challenges to what he later called \\"naive Keynesian\\" (as opposed to New Keynesian) theory began with his 1950s reinterpretation of the consumption function, and he became the main advocate opposing activist Keynesian government policies. In the late 1960s he described his own approach (along with all of mainstream economics) as using \\"Keynesian language and apparatus\\" yet rejecting its \\"initial\\" conclusions. During the 1960s he promoted an alternative macroeconomic policy known as \\"monetarism\\". He theorized there existed a \\"natural\\" rate of unemployment, and argued that governments could increase employment above this rate (e.g., by increasing aggregate demand) only at the risk of causing inflation to accelerate. He argued that the Phillips curve was not stable and predicted what would come to be known as stagflation (high unemployment with high inflation). Friedman argued that, given the existence of the Federal Reserve, a constant small expansion of the money supply was the only wise policy.  This obviously was proven wrong at The Clinton Administration.<br /><br /> The mess we're in now--his great depression--was caused by several acts of Congress.  Carter aided to the problem in 1980 when he passed the Monetary Control Act, which ended regulations that prevented banks on many kinds of deposits.  Reagan gave the another blow with the Garn-St. Germain Act of 1982, which relaxed restrictions on the kinds of loans banks could make.  And finally, the dissolution of the Glass-Seagal Act of 1933 which was overturned by Congress in 1999 under President Clinton so that Citibank and Travelers could merge--which was illegal until then, because banks could not use depositors money to invest in the market.<br /><br /> The result was an increasingly unregulated system in which banks were free to give in fully to the overconfidence that the quiet period had created. Debt soared, risks multiplied, and then, the housing bubble burst and brought the system down.<br /><br />  Mr Krugman then walks us, in plain and easy language, to the way forward.  \\"Our priority must be to get ourselves back on the path to growth;  every day that we lag behind normal production levels only adds to the astronomical economic loss of this depression.\\"  What we need for rapid, powerful recovery is precisely what got us out of The Great Depression--a burst of government spending to jump-start the economy.  \\"...your spending is my income.\\"<br /><br /> Arguing both sides of the economic policies: Keynes' 1936 work, General Theory of Employment, Interest and Money and Milton Friedman's ideas which are causing European economies to be austere and depressed growth (and we know how bad that is going), Friedman talks about inflation, the deficit, deregulation. Friedman addresses them head-on until he crushes his opponents and delivers the knockout blow.<br /><br /> I think this is a must read for everyone who wants to make a difference in the 2012 Presidential election.ic policies: Keynes' 1936 work, General Theory of Employment, Interest and Money and Milton Friedman's ideas which are causing European economies to be austere and depressed growth (and we know how bad that is going), Friedman talks about inflation, the deficit, deregulation. 0061743526|5.0|The best way I can think of to describe \\"To Kill A Mockingbird\\" is that Harper Lee has managed to take a period of time from the 1930's Deep South and transform that into a novel. From the reviews I've seen, the depth with which this novel is written is often understated. The setting and the characters are so well drawn, and done so subtly, that I couldn't help but be pulled in.<br /><br />I also find that reviews understate the scope of the story. Alot of the time people focus on the trial, and the theme of racism, but it's so much more than that. The story touches on issues of gender, class, and religion. It touches on compassion and empathy, and justice. But, as with all detailed, complex, and well-realised stories, there's a greater depth to things, despite how simple everything seems on the surface.<br /><br />There are two plots here. The first, introduced in the opening chapter, spans the whole novel. It's through this plot that the entire story moves. We get to see and experience life in Maycomb, as well as the children's quest to see Boo Radley. Basically, this plot is character-driven. This story is subtle; it's something you'll probably understand from the read, but will have to think about to articulate it.<br /><br />The second plot is the trial. While this is, essentially, the heart of the book, it couldn't be written this well without everything else. This is introduced subtly, and is built-up over the course of the story. This is really done quite well; Lee starts with small details, then the details and events get increasingly larger, more immediate, more important. More background is established, and everything is made clear. One event leads to another, and I was dragged along with it. Even when the narrative isn't focused on the trial, it has a purpose, albeit an indirect one. It's like easing into a warm bath.<br /><br />The writing is consistently tight. I don't recall seeing an unnecessary word, or a poorly written sentence, and I'm a pedant about these things. Everything on the page, every scene, has relevance; the amount of space given to it is, more or less, directly proportionate to the importance of what's happening. And even though long periods of time are often skipped or reduced into a paragraph or two, the shift in time is worked smoothly.<br /><br />The characterisation is also great. I found almost all the characters compelling in their own way. They're complex people, and almost all the character cast are sympathetic in one way or another. There's also alot of conflict, and moral dilemmas on top of that. All of the main characters are written excellently, with a resonating solidity. And when these characters are going through tough moments, and being torn apart inside, we're not beaten over the head with it. It's handled delicately, yet the narrative doesn't wallow in it, and Lee isn't concerned with emotional dramatics.<br /><br />And the social commentary is great. Some may complain about preaching to the choir, but I feel an ever-present reminder is appropriate given the frequency with which the message can be conveniently ignored. But again, we're not beaten over the head with the themes, they're just often present. Not to mention that the message is conveyed precisely, and with such an underlying strength to add to the book's power.<br /><br />Combine all these together, and you end up with a formidable book. I thoroughly enjoyed this, found the story engaging, and looking back my fondness of it only grows.<br /><br />5/5

B005LAIHZY|5.0|Pixar Animation Studios has always been known for their unique approach to filmmaking. They have always put the story first and foremost, something that so many other studios seem to take for granted. They also know the rest of the ingredients to add to make their films stand out from the rest: Great characters, lots of heart, and a goodly quantity of humor.<br /><br />While not continuing the 21st century tradition of turning the fairy-tale on its head, as in the case of \\"Shrek\\", \\"Brave\\", feels like a rediscovered story by Grimm's with an unmistakably Pixarian flavor. Legends and myths abound in Scots folklore, and the writers at Pixar didn't have far to go in order to come up with their own story, one that is at once relevant, quite original, and yet entirely congruous with one's expectations of the perfect fairy-tale. As fresh and surprising as any of the studio's previous successes, I enjoyed it immensely, and could watch it many times over without growing tired of it. It's hard to find a real family film that everyone can enjoy together, but this one comes pretty to close to that ideal.<br /><br />In the ultimate cautionary tale of being careful what you wish for, the film, set in 10th century Scotland, concerns Princess Merida, a feisty, strong-willed, adventure-seeking lass, skilled with both a sword and a bow and arrow, who would rather practice archery and ride through the woods with her horse, Angus, than follow the path desired for her by her parents; or more specifically, her mother. Determined to avoid her fate, she runs away, and manages to find a way to alter her destiny. To make a long story short, this ill-chosen wish sets off a chain of events that threaten to destroy everything Merida holds dear and the entire kingdom of DunBroch along with it.<br /><br />This fresh take on a typical princess character pleasantly surprised me, as well as the fact that the emotional core of the film, as well as most of the plot, stems not from a romantic relationship between prince and princess, but from the mother/daughter relationship between Merida and Queen Elinor. Anyone who is a daughter like I am will easily relate to how complicated the bond can be between a mother and her child, and this film portrays that unique bond beautifully. Without giving too much away, it is their love for each other and their clashing of personalities that ultimately moves the entire film forward, and the changes they both experience throughout the course of the film give them both a better understanding of each other, and emphasize the importance of empathy, bravery, courage, family, and love.<br /><br />Not content with just being an entertaining theatrical experience, the film goes into a level of moral and emotional depth that even fans of Pixar may be unfamiliar with. It takes a very special film to bring me to tears, and I was moved quite often. Not to say that \\"Brave\\" is strictly a tear-jerker, because it is not. I'd estimate that there is more action, adventure, and humor than in any other Pixar movie to date.<br /><br />There is no doubt that \\"Brave\\" is one of Pixar's most beautifully animated films. The amount of detail is simply brilliant, and computer technology being what it is today, there are times when the visuals on screen almost rival the real world. I've never been to Scotland, but the richness and beauty of the country expressed in the film makes me feel as if I had. The landscapes are simply breathtaking, and so photo-realistic you might feel as if you've just fallen into the wilds of the Highlands; I'm sure that more than one viewing would be needed to absorb it all. The rendering on the hair and fabric is especially lifelike, and certain scenes involving non-human characters might make you do a double-take, they appear so true-to-life!<br /><br />Composer Patrick Doyle's score is appropriately Scottish in flavor, combining the expected Gaelic melodies (jigs, reels, and such like), with more modern, atmospheric, incidental scoring. Sprightly where it needs to be and brooding where it doesn't, it perfectly enhances the movie to the point that it feels as organic as the moss on the trees. The gentle lullaby, \\"A Mhaighdean Bhan Uasal\\", sung by Emma Thompson, is one of the stand-out pieces, and serves as the kind of musical representation of the love between Merida and Elinor. I also quite enjoyed \\"Song of Mor'du\\", a sort of Scotch drinking song, heard mainly in the background, but reiterated often throughout the rest of the score. There are also three other songs: \\"Touch The Sky\\" and \\"Into The Open Air\\", performed by Scots songstress Julie Fowlis, and \\"Learn Me Right\\", sung by Birdy, with Mumford and Sons, which, while not written by Doyle, fit expertly into their places in the story and aptly enhance the film.<br /><br />Now, a good animated character needs a good voice to bring them to life, and the entire cast is all very well-chosen and very well-acted. Comprised mainly of Scottish and English actors, including Billy Connolly as King Fergus, Emma Thompson as Queen Elinor, Robbie Coltrane as Lord Dingwall, Julie Walters as the Witch, and the incomparable Kelly Macdonald as Merida herself, it would be hard to find another group of people so perfect in their roles. Macdonald is wonderful as the flame-haired heroine, and Thompson simply shines as the equally obstinate but caring mother. Billy Connolly is a great counterpoint to her more level-headed and dutiful Queen, but gets far less screen time than either of the two female protagonists.<br /><br />To sum it all up, \\"Brave\\" is a beautiful, touching, exciting, and refreshingly original film, one that is entertaining on so many levels and can stand proudly alongside any of Pixar's previous masterpieces. I would rank it as one of my favorite (if not my favorite) Pixar film, and would not hesitate to recommend it to anyone.

0547258305|4.0|I can see why Graceling is so well-loved by so many people. The writing is fantastic, and the world-building exquisite. I love the idea of this fantasy world where certain people have what essentially amounts to mutant powers. The notion of the different-colored eyes marking the Graced is great, as is the exploration of how the inhabitants of the different kingdoms view the Graced. In one kingdom, the Graced are honored, where in another, they are shamed. It's a very subtle look at prejudices and stereotypes woven throughout the plot, and how those perceptions impact an individual's self-image, and I thought it was very well done.<br /><br />The plot was also lovely. I enjoyed the action, and although I've read several reviews that thought the pacing was slow and the length ponderous, I thought it moved rather quickly. Maybe that's because most of the fantasy I read is adult. This book was certainly longer than a lot of YA fare, but I thought the length was justified by the story.<br /><br />Po was a fantastic character. I liked him immediately. I loved that he was nuanced and flawed, and I was surprised along with Katsa when new facts were revealed about him. There are certain parts of the book where Po is not present, and while they are extremely exciting and tense, I was still slightly distracted wishing Po was there. It's always fun when a book makes me actually miss a character when he's not around.<br /><br />I also loved the character of Princess Bitterblue, who is the focal point of one of the companion novels. While she was a child, I admired her attitude and spunk, and I enjoyed reading about her.<br /><br />I did have a few issues with the book, and these were just matters of preference, not of the storytelling or the writing. I was not a huge fan of Katsa. I understood why she was the way she was, and I definitely acknowledge that she is a far cry from many of the helpless damsels in distress that are abundant in YA literature. However, her extremely guarded and untrusting nature didn't make her a character I really enjoyed reading about. Don't get me wrong - I enjoyed the story that she was involved in, I just didn't really enjoy her. And although she does exhibit some growth during the course of the book, it wasn't enough to make me really like her by the end.<br /><br />I also was a bit let down by the climax of the book (which actually occurs several chapters before the ending). It seemed kind of lacking after so much build-up. Now, I'm not entirely sure how it could have been done better or differently; I just know that after I finished reading it, my thought was, \\"Oh, that was it?\\"<br /><br />However, as an overall story, Graceling excelled. And considering neither of the companion novels focus on Katsa, I'm extremely interested in reading more about this beautiful fantasy world and the amazing characters that populate it.rded and untrusting nature didn't make her a character I really enjoyed reading about. B0080R7Q70|5.0|Banks of Eden, to this reviewer, is probably one of the better Flower Kings releases I have heard in about the past decade. Keeping in mind that this is the only release in the last five years. But, I think the time off has really done them a lot of good. They seemed to have stepped back from the machine like rhythm they got themselves into. You could expect a new release every year. Getting apart from one another, working on independent projects, and tours seems to have really refreshed them.<br /><br />All of the numbers are enthusiastically played and where the Flower Kings can seldom be accused of \\"going through the motions\\" there seems to be something that just sounds fresh and renewed about their performance. Dare I say that some of their recent work was getting boring?<br />One of the best things to come about during their recording hiatus is their new drummer Felix Lehrmann. Definitely the best drummer they have had since  Jaime Salazar and may be the best they have ever had. While not as heavy handed as someone like Mike Portnoy, he has a similar style where drumming becomes much more than just keeping a beat. He fills in holes and adds runs and rhythm changes; making a significant and exciting change to their rhythm section. The strongest compliment I can give a drummer is that you notice them! You hear them doing something special and you concentrate on listening just to them. Felix is this type of drummer. When you add this to Jonas Reingold's superior bass work you now have one of the most exciting rhythm sections in progressive music. Missing is Hasse Bruniusson on percussion, but dare I say with Felix he is not missed. (Sorry) Even though Roine Stolt's guitar playing has always been impressive, his many solos on Banks of Eden are fresh, sharp, and often stunning. I don't realy hear Tomas Bodin's keyboards taking much of a front seat this time around. He is there, but I can't recall him standing out in any particular song. Roine and Hasse Froberg freely exchange guitar solos throught BOE especially on \\"Numbers\\". I would say it does sound like a \\"guitar driven\\" album.<br /><br />With the stellar first song \\"Numbers\\" (at 25:20) taking up the first half of the CD the remaining numbers are quite a bit shorter. The remaining 4 are just 6 or 7 minutes long. The shorter numbers keep the second half of the cd moving right along and as most Flower Kings CD's, it ends before you really want it to. Even the songs on the bonus disk are quite good. While these tracks tend to become throwaway these could all actually stand on their own on another release. These 4 are all short as well with the longest just over 6 minutes. Speaking of the bonus disk, the interview, especially the vintage instrument and equipment tour is priceless, not to mention pretty humorous!<br /><br />All in all, you will really get nothing different from \\"Banks of Eden\\" than any other FK release. They really do not go out on any limbs and experiment with anything new. They stay with their tried and true formula and sound. What they may lack in originality on this CD they make up for it with enthusiasm! But for all who like just a good ol' FK cd this will fit the bill, will not disappoint, and remind us why we like them so much! I have to give it 5 stars in comparison to their last several releases and give kudos to Felix. While having absolutely nothing to do with the music, the artwork is stellar. Definitley some of the nicest artwork I have seen in some time!

0393088774|1.0|There's nothing new in Paul Krugman's new book. It's the same old prescription to print, borrow, tax, and spend more and more with no plan to stop. Krugman pretends to know all the answers to our economic problems. He admits a depression but fails to point out that our administration officially called the recession at an end in 2009. In a prior book, 'The Return of Depression Economics'  he equivocated by saying that the world is not in a depression, but he's not quite sure. Now, after three years of the Obama administration, he's sure. Krugman, like the politicians he supports, would like to do more and more when it is apparent that it can accomplish less and less.<br /><br />Immediately after repudiating money as the proper vehicle of measurement Krugman cites the German hyperinflation of the early 1920s. His economic amateurism shows as he treats his readership like idiots. He thinks that we believe Hitler came to power as a result of the German inflation of 1923. The German inflation ran from 1920 to 1923 when Hjalmar Schacht instituted monetary reforms of the kind that Krugman deplores. The German economy then did quite well until overcome by the world wide depression that brought Hitler into power. He does point out that the inflation wiped out middle class savings, a terrifying reminder of what is happening in the USA today, albeit currently slower than was the result of similar Wiemar social policy.<br /><br />Krugman claims to follow Keynes' legacy, but his understanding of Keynes is suspect. Keynes would be horrified to see his ideas used to cover for permanent deficits in good times and bad. Keynes would be the first to point out that his multiplier does not include pork and entitlement spending, nor welfare items like unemployment extensions. Keynes applied a metaphor of magneto trouble to the British economy of 1929. Krugman is unoriginal enough to apply Keynes's metaphor to the current American problem. It is doubtful that Keynes would see it the sameway. Our defects are built into our political structure.  The engine is broken; it's not just the 'magneto.' Krugman decries the current animus against Keynes. Keynes' ideas are not the problem.  The fault is in politicians who are cheered on by misinterpretations of spending advocates. QE stimulus is so full of well meaning social spending as well as pork and corruption that few jobs are created, while cutting existing military placement will result in a net subtraction.<br /><br />The worst of Krugman's blatant errors is his insistence of the utility of printing money. He cites the simplistic model of a babysitting coop that got out of a coupon savings glut by printing coupons.  Overly fascinated with the successful baby sitting co-op described by Joan and Richard Sweeney, Krugman uses it as a simplistic model for the current U.S. economy. He neglects to observe that co-op members cannot invest their holdings and are not subject to government regulation, taxation, subsidies or stimulus. By the end of the book he has parlayed the babysitting metaphor into a recommendation for central planning while claiming that there really will be a free lunch from new ideas. It's frightening to see a liberal economist, of the type now firmly in control of government, thinking of money as coupons. The substance or source of these ideas remains undefined. A major premise is that rolling the printing press is a simple solution. As H.L. Mencken said, \\"For every complex problem there is a solution that is easy, simple and wrong.\\"<br /><br />In attempting to whitewash the burgeoning national debt relative to GDP he claims it's due to declining GDP. But GDP varies by a much small percentage than debt has been rising annually. He shows the debt/GDP ratio in a figure, but omits to show a related GDP curve. About four times in the book he illustrates his case with rising employment due to government spending in time of war. He appears to hope for a disaster as an opportunity for more government control of our personal and private resources. He fails to analyze the results of the guns and butter spending of the Viet Nam war era. The national debt, mostly hidden, is unsustainable for much longer. Krugman points out that debt can be a good thing, as for infrastructure, or housing or transportation. It's disingenuous to imply that's how our government debt has been used.<br /><br />He also makes the error of saying that the national debt is something that we owe among ourselves. That may have been true when Lincoln said it but now the Chinese, Japanese and British are now financing half of our deficits through an ever worsening balance of trade. The recent ruling by Tim Geithner granting direct Treasury access, heretofore reserved for US citizens, to China makes it a foolish remark for 2012.  It's one of the structural problems that Krugman fails to notice. It's highly disturbing to see an influential economist as a proponent of unlimited debt with no intention of payback. It amounts to government thievery, rather than welfare altruism or economic necessity.<br /><br />After an explanation of bank runs, Krugman takes a wrong turn as a movie critic.<br />George (Jimmy Stewart) in 'It's a Wonderful Life' averted a run in a flash back sponsored by his guardian angel, not as the author says an anachronism of a bank run after WWII.<br /><br />Krugman  justifies spending because the government can currently borrow at a low rate of interest.  What happens when interest rates rise? Where was he while all that spending was going on in times of higher interest rates? When interest rates rise due to either an improved economy, inflation mitigation, or government freezing out the private sector will liberals advise reduced spending?  As Jeanne Sahadi, CNNMoney.com, says, \\"Interest rates on U.S. bonds may be ridiculously low, but that doesn't mean the country's future interest payments on the national debt will be.\\" True, borrowing costs are currently low. Debt service now about 9% of GDP will go 40% as rates rise as they must after all the printing. In citing an excess supply of savings does Krugman mean foreigners? He says that he \\"doesn't want to minimize debt problems\\" then proceeds to do just that. He says the burden on future generations is smaller than stated by deficit hawks. Indeed devaluing money is a huge problem for current generation of savers and retirees. Our progeny will carry our debt, until default with cheaper money. Never mind that their salaries will be cheapened also. He proposes strange arithmetic in saying that it's ok if debt grows more slowly than the sum of inflation plus growth. It's not happening. Inflation, by government calculation is about 2.5% with growth about the same. The national debt has increased by 300% in three years. Krugman makes a good argument against himself<br /><br />It's ridiculous to say that the market is shouting that there is no worry about the deficit. What is Krugman looking at?  He denigrates the position of Pimco's Bill Gross, who voices concern over the creditworthiness of USA  bonds due to burgeoning debt. Gross cares about bondholders, Krugman does not. He says actual investors are unconcerned about the national debt. Is Gross not an actual investor? In fact he speaks for many. Krugman ignores our negative current account balance and excessive payroll tax burden on American labor. His anti- business bias shows as he cites borrowers like Lehman using repos. For your information Paul, GE and IBM also use repos. He says the GOP favors a return to the unsuccessful gold standard. A hog manure standard would be an improvement over the current unlimited fiat money based on debt.<br /><br />Krugman asks if consumption is down and government spending is down who buys our bonds or where is the money coming from? It's coming from Chinese and other foreign investors who are financing our debt. He ignores the detrimental effect of our permanently negative current account balance.He asks is our problem is structural. Yes it is. He overuses what he calls a 'Minsky moment.'  He should consider the potential tipping point when increased government debt passes the point when there is no more confidence in US bonds. As Margarete Thatcher said \\"The trouble with socialism is that eventually you run out of other peoples money.\\" Krugman doesn't say how much is enough or when to stop. He does say that this is not the right time to slow down spending so his answer seems to be never, never , never.<br /><br />Citing the anatomy of an inadequate response, Krugman points out that there is currently a world wide depression. He ignores China and Germany. He says the \\"obvious answer\\" is fiscal stimulus. Obviously not! Germany is doing quite well while Obama and Hillary continue to inveigle Merkel for more stimulus. Merkel recognizes that most of the stimulus benefit of American spending is going offshore. QE1 and QE2 spending is not enough. Increasing spending in 2007-2010 was, by admission, to help poor and near poor, not for economic stimulation. Krugman courageously references Rienhard and Rogoff 'This Time It's Different'. That book is a good refutation of Krugman's ideas. (7)<br /><br />Inequality is not the cause of our current economic mess and inflation is understated by our government. For a PhD, Krugman is remarkably naive if he ignores monetary dilution to believe the moderate current inflation figure around 3%. CPI and PPI figures, used to measured \\"Core\\" inflation, are manipulated by excluding volatile components each month. The flood of money that holds interest rates low can't fail to create price inflation in the future.<br />Krugman calls inflation a phantom menace. Tell that to a retiree who needs his savings to support  his retirement. He will be a government dependent before his 20 year retirement is finished. But that conforms with Obama and Krugman's vision of equality. Krugman ignores the many retirees attempting to live off of lifetime savings in an increasingly futile attempt to avoid dependence on government largess. A recent CNN article points to one third of the middle class dropping into poverty since implementation of the social programs of the 1960s. The trend is accelerating for the rest of us.  (9)<br /><br />Krugman says the large European trade deficit needs plausible path back to being competetive. What about the USA? Why is Krugman's advice so different for the USA than for Europe? He spends much space justifying why the picture doesn't show what it seems. Spending won't solve problems for Europe or the USA.<br /><br />Asking what it will take to get out of the current malaise, Krugman suggests \\"spend now pay later,\\" \\"vote for hope\\" and \\"Rooseveltian resolve,\\" all  wishful thinking. True, the economy is not improving by itself. It's also evident that QE1 and QE2 have not helped. Krugman is critical of Fed Chairman Bernanke even though the Fed has tripled the monetary base since 2008. (12)<br /><br />One accurate statement that Krugman makes is that the time for austerity is in boom times, not recession. Did he advocate cutting back on the LBJ guns and butter approach that killed the boom in the 1960s? Did he attempt to slow the Clinton spending of surpluses in the 1990s? It's certainly true that if that money was available today we could spend our way out of a weak economic cycle. No one thinks that spending cutbacks will invoke prosperity, but there needs to be a recognition that government stimulus is not working. It can only lead to disaster for our progeny down the road. It's fair to say that intervention worked in the '30s, but hasn't worked since. In his concluding chapter, entitled 'End this Depression,' Krugman cites austerity mania. What austerity mania? The only agreement in effect is a 1.5 trillion cut over ten years. With a current 15 trillion debt, it won't even slow down spending. Krugman never says how much is enough or when to stop. It's a sad situation when austerity (savings) becomes a dirty word. Economics, in the author's words, has become a morality play. (13)<br /><br />Mostly we see an economist trying to morph into a social scientist. In spite of denial, Krugman mixes economics with politics, comparing Romney to Gordon Gecko in the movie 'Wall Street' and saying that Obama is most likely to do the right thing. His economics is often based on aphorisms like \\"ketchup economists\\" and \\"schlock economics\\".  The book is devoid of new ideas or logic. For a better view of the liberal position read Joseph Stiglitz or Robert Eisner instead.

B002IKLZZY|5.0|Roger O. Thornhill is a busy New York City advertising executive who loves his mother and loves his women. He's having an after-work drink with three buddies when he's abducted at gunpoint. They take him to a mansion in the country, and their boss, Lester Townsend, scrutinizes his captive: &quot;Not what I expected. A little taller. A little more polished than the others.&quot;<br /><br />Thornhill replies, &quot;Not that I mind a slight case of abduction now and then, but I have tickets to the theater this evening. To a show I was looking forward to!&quot;  And that sets the pace for the whole movie. Politely sinister. Classy humor. Thornhill is played with panache and great comic touch by Cary Grant. Townsend is played with sly menace by James Mason. The lovely Eva Marie Saint doesn't even appear in the 1st half of the movie, then her Eve Kendall takes over the screen and Thornhill's heart - but it costs her.<br /><br />&quot;North by Northwest&quot; has a couple of the most recognized and parodied scenes in films. The cropduster chasing Cary Grant in the middle of nowhere never loses its fear factor. And who can forget Roger Thornhill and Eve Kendall climbing down Mt. Rushmore?<br /><br />I'm writing this review to let you know that the extras on the 50th Anniversary Edition, 2 DVD's, are simply excellent<br />[[ASIN:B002IKLZZY North by Northwest (Two-Disc 50th Anniversary Edition)]]:<br /><br />1.  &quot;Cary Grant: A Class Apart&quot;. It is narrated by Helen Mirren and Jeremy Northam, with many movie clips and contributing commenters. Cary Grant was in 72 films, including 4 directed by Alfred Hitchcock. He ended his film career voluntarily, quitting while he was at the top. He passed away in 1986 of a stroke. Look at this list of interviewees for this special! This was also fascinating to watch. B006X3VNJK|5.0|Many of the currently re-released and so called \\"remastered\\" classic albums from days of yore sound worse than the originals did, due, in large part, to large amounts of compression that the studios are now employing. This compression squishes the dynamics, robbing the listener of the 'high fidelity' sound that the original typically has, in many cases. Happily, this is not the case with Donald Fagen's The Nightfly, which was recorded, mixed, and mastered extremely well on its original release LP, providing an excellent base (in the original master tapes) from which to start. This remaster not only rivals the sonics of the original album, but the vinyl surface of the remaster is so quiet, it could ~almost~ pass for that of a CD. (In the spirit of full disclosure, I must include the fact that the original LP I ordered had a tremendous sonic glitch about 45 seconds into Track 1, Side 1, and had to be returned for exchange. The replacement, however, had all the wonderful characteristics of which I speak.) If you like this album and you like vinyl, buy this - it's great!

Other than some dropouts along the coast (where cell service is questionable), there were no lost connections as I streamed music via Plex to my iPhone in the van with Bluetooth and that's a 6-hour drive. 1781161097|4.0|This was a very well presented 'Art of' book for Ridley Scott's Prometheus. It has some wonderful shots of the pre-production designs as well as behind the scenes photos of the sets and filming. However, like many newer books of its ilk, it it flawed. It does not showcase the entire production from beginning to end, and by that I mean concepts that were dropped or alternate avenues of exploration considered, especially since much of the story was being rewritten at the time. It was like a PR person went to town on it. Instead, it only showcases the choice pieces, those that only made it into the film.<br /><br />One such design (Spoilers ahead!) was the Deacon, the final design for the chestburster alien. The book only has six photos or so of the sculpture and prop they used, and even then only a few of the actual concept designs. Where are the drawings? Surely the design team made more than six perfect designs. They go into great detail explaining the process, such as taking inspiration from the goblin shark, but do little in actually showing it. I had to Google most of the designs that never saw print. And let me tell you, they are far and away different than the ones in the book!<br /><br />When you consider the original Alien movie, as well as the original 'making of' and Giger's Alien books, they explored a lot of dropped designs. For me, an 'Art of' book should have the entire process from the failed to the successful. After all, it's the journey that's the most satisfying, not the destination.<br /><br />Having said my piece, don't let it dissuade you from purchasing this book. It made a fine addition to my collection as well as, I'm sure, your own. I do recommend it.

B005HP2J66|3.0|Werner Herzog is given intimate access to the earliest instance of cave painting known to modern man, the Chauvet Cave in southern France. It's a mesmerizing topic, but after the third or fourth time the screen pans dramatically across the same six sketches, it's clear that elaboration just isn't in the cards. Rather than using these caves as a jumping-off point to discuss early man and his habit of cave painting in general, Herzog stands in place and explores the particulars of these specific illustrations. Ultimately he doesn't, though, and his long, dull conversations with the local team of researchers only further belabor a struggling theme. Dry and emotionless as a textbook with just one or two moments of wonder, it's too much for a TV episode and too little for a feature-length documentary.
